      <p>
       <b>
        Occitan
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <small>
         English:
        </small>
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">
            ɒ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">
            ən
           </span>
          </span>
          , -
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
          </span>
          , -
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">
            ɑː
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <small>
        Occitan:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Occitan" title="Help:IPA/Occitan">
         [utsiˈta]
        </a>
       </span>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <small>
        French:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">
         [ɔksitɑ̃]
        </a>
       </span>
       ), also known as
       <i>
        <b>
         lenga d'òc
        </b>
       </i>
       (
       <small>
        Occitan:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Occitan" title="Help:IPA/Occitan">
         [ˈleŋɡɔ ˈðɔ(k)]
        </a>
       </span>
       <small class="nowrap">
        (
        <span class="unicode haudio">
         <span class="fn">
          <span style="white-space:nowrap">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Oc-Lenga_d%27%C3%B2c.ogg" title="About this sound">
            <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
           </a>
          </span>
          <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Oc-Lenga_d%27%C3%B2c.ogg" title="Oc-Lenga d'òc.ogg">
           listen
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
        )
       </small>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         langue d'oc
        </span>
       </i>
       ) by its native speakers, is a
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       . It is spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">
        southern France
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_Valleys" title="Occitan Valleys">
        Occitan Valleys
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">
        Monaco
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Val_d%27Aran" title="Val d'Aran">
        Val d'Aran
       </a>
       ; collectively, these regions are sometimes referred to as
       <a href="/wiki/Occitania" title="Occitania">
        Occitania
       </a>
       . Occitan is also spoken in the linguistic enclave of
       <a href="/wiki/Guardia_Piemontese" title="Guardia Piemontese">
        Guardia Piemontese
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">
        Calabria
       </a>
       , Italy). However, there is controversy about the unity of the language, as some think that Occitan is a
       <a href="/wiki/ISO_639_macrolanguage" title="ISO 639 macrolanguage">
        macrolanguage
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Others include
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       in this family, as the
       <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_distance" title="Linguistic distance">
        distance
       </a>
       between this language and some Occitan dialects (such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Gascon_language" title="Gascon language">
        Gascon language
       </a>
       ) is similar to the distance among different Occitan dialects. In fact, Catalan was considered an Occitan dialect until the end of the 19th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Today, Occitan is an official language in
       <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">
        Catalonia
       </a>
       , where a subdialect of Gascon known as
       <a href="/wiki/Aranese_dialect" title="Aranese dialect">
        Aranese
       </a>
       is spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Val_d%27Aran" title="Val d'Aran">
        Val d'Aran
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Occitan's closest relative is
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since September 2010, the
       <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Catalonia" title="Parliament of Catalonia">
        Parliament of Catalonia
       </a>
       has considered Aranese Occitan to be the officially preferred language for use in the
       <a href="/wiki/Val_d%27Aran" title="Val d'Aran">
        Val d'Aran
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Across history, the terms
       <a href="/wiki/Limousin_dialect" title="Limousin dialect">
        Limousin
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Lemosin
       </i>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Languedocien_dialect" title="Languedocien dialect">
        Languedocien
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Lengadocian
       </i>
       ),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gascon_dialect" title="Gascon dialect">
        Gascon
       </a>
       , and later
       <a href="/wiki/Proven%C3%A7al_dialect" title="Provençal dialect">
        Provençal
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Provençal
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Provençau
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Prouvençau
       </i>
       ) have been used as synonyms for the whole of Occitan; nowadays, "Provençal" is understood mainly as the Occitan dialect spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">
        Provence
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike other Romance languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , there is no single written standard language called "Occitan", and Occitan has no official status in France, home to most of Occitania. Instead, there are competing norms for writing Occitan, some of which attempt to be pan-dialectal, whereas others are based on particular dialects (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Proven%C3%A7al_dialect" title="Provençal dialect">
        Provençal
       </a>
       in southeast France, or
       <a href="/wiki/Gascon_language" title="Gascon language">
        Gascon
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Val_d%27Aran" title="Val d'Aran">
        Val d'Aran
       </a>
       of Spain, where it is known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aranese_language" title="Aranese language">
        Aranese
       </a>
       ). These efforts are hindered by the rapidly declining use of Occitan as a spoken language in much of southern France, as well as by the significant differences in phonology and vocabulary between different Occitan dialects.
      </p>
      <p>
       In particular, the northern and easternmost dialects have more morphological and phonetic features in common with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Italian_languages" title="Northern Italian languages">
        Gallo-Italic
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O%C3%AFl_languages" title="Oïl languages">
        Oïl languages
       </a>
       (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">
        nasal vowels
       </a>
       ; loss of final consonants; initial
       <i>
        cha/ja-
       </i>
       instead of
       <i>
        ca/ga-
       </i>
       ;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uvular_R" title="Uvular R">
        uvular ⟨r⟩
       </a>
       ; the front-rounded sound
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ø/
       </span>
       instead of a diphthong,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /w/
       </span>
       instead of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /l/
       </span>
       before a consonant), whereas the southernmost dialects have more features in common with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibero-Romance_languages" title="Ibero-Romance languages">
        Ibero-Romance languages
       </a>
       (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Betacism" title="Betacism">
        betacism
       </a>
       ; voiced fricatives between vowels in place of voiced stops; -
       <i>
        ch
       </i>
       - in place of -
       <i>
        it
       </i>
       -), and Gascon has a number of unusual features not seen in other dialects (e.g.
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /h/
       </span>
       in place of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /f/
       </span>
       ; loss of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       between vowels; intervocalic
       <i>
        -r-
       </i>
       and final
       <i>
        -t/ch
       </i>
       in place of medieval -
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        ll
       </span>
       -). There are also significant lexical differences, where some dialects have words cognate with French, and others have Catalan and Spanish cognates (
       <i>
        maison
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        casa
       </i>
       "house",
       <i>
        testa
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        cap
       </i>
       "head",
       <i>
        petit
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        pichon
       </i>
       "small",
       <i>
        achaptar
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        crompar
       </i>
       "to buy",
       <i>
        entendre
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        ausir
       </i>
       "to hear",
       <i>
        se taire
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        se calar
       </i>
       "to be quiet",
       <i>
        tombar
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        caire
       </i>
       "to fall",
       <i>
        p(l)us
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        mai
       </i>
       "more",
       <i>
        totjorn
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        sempre
       </i>
       "always", etc.). Nonetheless, there is a significant amount of
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        mutual intelligibility
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The long-term survival of Occitan is in grave doubt. According to the
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Red_Book_of_Endangered_Languages" title="Red Book of Endangered Languages">
        Red Book of Endangered Languages
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       four of the six major dialects of Occitan (Provençal,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auvergnat_dialect" title="Auvergnat dialect">
        Auvergnat
       </a>
       , Limousin and Languedocien) are considered
       <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language#Defining_and_measuring_endangerment" title="Endangered language">
        severely endangered
       </a>
       , whereas the remaining two (
       <a href="/wiki/Gascon_language" title="Gascon language">
        Gascon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vivaro-Alpine_dialect" title="Vivaro-Alpine dialect">
        Vivaro-Alpine
       </a>
       ) are considered
       <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language#Defining_and_measuring_endangerment" title="Endangered language">
        definitely endangered
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Name">
        Name
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_modern_term">
        History of the modern term
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History of the modern term">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carta_Occitania.pdf">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1003" data-file-width="1181" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Carta_Occitania.pdf/page1-250px-Carta_Occitania.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Carta_Occitania.pdf/page1-375px-Carta_Occitania.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Carta_Occitania.pdf/page1-500px-Carta_Occitania.pdf.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carta_Occitania.pdf" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Main cities of Occitania, written in the Occitan language
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The name Occitan comes from
       <i>
        lenga d'òc
       </i>
       ("language of òc"),
       <i>
        òc
       </i>
       being the Occitan word for
       <i>
        yes.
       </i>
       While the term would have been in use orally for some time after the decline of Latin, as far as historical records show, the Italian medieval poet
       <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
        Dante
       </a>
       was the first to have recorded the term
       <i>
        lingua d'oc
       </i>
       in writing. In his
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/De_vulgari_eloquentia" title="De vulgari eloquentia">
         De vulgari eloquentia
        </a>
       </i>
       , he wrote in Latin, "
       <i>
        nam alii oc, alii si, alii vero dicunt oil
       </i>
       " ("for some say
       <i>
        òc
       </i>
       , others
       <i>
        sì
       </i>
       , yet others say
       <i>
        oïl
       </i>
       "), thereby highlighting three major
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance literary languages
       </a>
       that were well known in Italy, based on each language's word for "yes", the
       <i>
        òc language
       </i>
       (Occitan), the
       <i>
        oïl language
       </i>
       (French), and the
       <i>
        sì language
       </i>
       (Sicilian and Italian). This was not, of course, the only defining characteristic of each group.
      </p>
      <p>
       The word
       <i>
        òc
       </i>
       came from
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       <i>
        hoc
       </i>
       ("this"), while
       <i>
        oïl
       </i>
       originated from Latin
       <i>
        hoc illud
       </i>
       ("this [is] it").
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Catalan" title="Old Catalan">
        Old Catalan
       </a>
       , and now the Catalan of Northern Catalonia (
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         Catalunya Nord
        </span>
       </i>
       ) also have
       <i>
        hoc
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        òc
       </i>
       ). Other Romance languages derive their word for "yes" from the Latin
       <i>
        sic,
       </i>
       "thus [it is], [it was done], etc.", such as Spanish
       <i>
        sí
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Lombard_dialect" title="Eastern Lombard dialect">
        Eastern Lombard
       </a>
       <i>
        sé
       </i>
       , Sicilian and Italian
       <i>
        sì
       </i>
       , or Portuguese
       <i>
        sim
       </i>
       . In Modern Catalan, as in modern Spanish,
       <i>
        sí
       </i>
       is usually used as a response, although the language retains the word
       <i>
        oi
       </i>
       , akin to
       <i>
        òc
       </i>
       , which is sometimes used at the end of
       <a href="/wiki/Yes%E2%80%93no_question" title="Yes–no question">
        yes–no questions
       </a>
       , and also in higher register as a positive response.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       French uses
       <i>
        si
       </i>
       to answer "yes" in response to questions that are asked in the negative sense: e.g., "Vous n'avez pas de frères?" "Si, j'en ai sept." ("You have no brothers?" "Yes [I do], I have seven.").
      </p>
      <p>
       The name "Occitan" was attested around 1300 as "occitanus", a crossing of
       <i>
        oc
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        aquitanus
       </i>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquitania" title="Aquitania">
        Aquitanian
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_names_for_Occitan">
        Other names for Occitan
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Other names for Occitan">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       For many centuries, the Occitan dialects (together with
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       were referred to as
       <i>
        Limousin
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Provençal
       </i>
       , the names of two regions lying within modern Occitania. After
       <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Mistral" title="Frédéric Mistral">
        Frédéric Mistral
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9librige" title="Félibrige">
        Félibrige
       </a>
       movement in the 19th century, Provençal achieved the greatest literary recognition and so became the most popular term for Occitan.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Anglade" title="Joseph Anglade">
        Joseph Anglade
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">
        philologist
       </a>
       and specialist of
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        medieval
       </a>
       literature who helped impose the then
       <a href="/wiki/Archaism" title="Archaism">
        archaic
       </a>
       term
       <i>
        Occitan
       </i>
       as the sole correct name,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the word
       <i>
        Lemosin
       </i>
       was first used to designate the language at the beginning of the 13th century by
       <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">
        Catalan
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Troubadour" title="Troubadour">
        troubadour
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Raimon_Vidal_de_Bezaudun" title="Raimon Vidal de Bezaudun">
        Raimon Vidal de Besalú
       </a>
       in his
       <i>
        Razós de trobar
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        <i>
         La parladura Francesca val mais et [es] plus avinenz a far romanz e pasturellas; mas cella de Lemozin val mais per far vers et cansons et serventés; et per totas las terras de nostre lengage son de major autoritat li cantar de la lenga Lemosina que de negun'autra parladura, per qu'ieu vos en parlarai primeramen.
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnglade1921[http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k255748j/f43.image_7]_20-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnglade1921[http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k255748j/f43.image_7]-20">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
       <p>
        The French language is worthier and better suited for romances and
        <a href="/wiki/Pastourelle" title="Pastourelle">
         pastourelles
        </a>
        ; but the language from
        <a href="/wiki/History_of_Limousin" title="History of Limousin">
         Limousin
        </a>
        is of greater value for writing poems and
        <a href="/wiki/Canso_(song)" title="Canso (song)">
         cançons
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Sirventes" title="Sirventes">
         sirventés
        </a>
        ; and across the whole of the lands where our tongue is spoken, the literature in the Limousin language has more authority than any other dialect, wherefore I shall use this name in priority.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       As for the word
       <i>
        Provençal
       </i>
       , it should not be taken as strictly meaning the language of
       <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">
        Provence
       </a>
       , but of
       <a href="/wiki/Occitania" title="Occitania">
        Occitania
       </a>
       as a whole, for "in the eleventh, the twelfth, and sometimes also the thirteenth centuries, one would understand under the name of Provence the whole territory of the old
       <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Narbonensis" title="Gallia Narbonensis">
        Provincia Romana
       </a>
       and even
       <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Aquitania" title="Gallia Aquitania">
        Aquitaine
       </a>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The term first came into fashion in
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Currently,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguist" title="Linguist">
        linguists
       </a>
       use the terms "Provençal" and "Limousin" strictly to refer to specific varieties within Occitania, keeping the name "Occitan" for the language as a whole. Many non-specialists, however, continue to refer to the language as Provençal, causing some confusion.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Occitan" title="Old Occitan">
        Old Occitan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_literature" title="Occitan literature">
        Occitan literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       One of the oldest written fragments of the language ever found dates back to the year 960, in an official text that was mixed with Latin:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        De ista hora in antea non DECEBRÀ Ermengaus filius Eldiarda Froterio episcopo filio Girberga NE Raimundo filio Bernardo vicecomite de castello de Cornone... NO·L LI TOLRÀ NO·L LI DEVEDARÀ NI NO L'EN DECEBRÀ... nec societatem non AURÀ, si per castellum recuperare NON O FA, et si recuperare potuerit in potestate Froterio et Raimundo LO TORNARÀ, per ipsas horas quæ Froterius et Raimundus L'EN COMONRÀ.
        <i>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
          <a href="#cite_note-23">
           [23]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </i>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
        Carolingian
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Litany" title="Litany">
        litanies
       </a>
       (ca 780), both written and sung in
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       , were
       <a href="/wiki/Response_(liturgy)" title="Response (liturgy)">
        answered
       </a>
       to in Old Occitan by the audience (
       <i>
        Ora pro nos
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        Tu lo juva
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Other famous pieces include the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Boecis" title="Boecis">
         Boecis
        </a>
       </i>
       , a 258-line-long poem written entirely in the Limousin dialect of Occitan between the year 1000 and 1030 and inspired by
       <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">
        Boethius
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Consolation_of_Philosophy" title="The Consolation of Philosophy">
         The Consolation of Philosophy
        </a>
       </i>
       ; the
       <a href="/wiki/Waldensians" title="Waldensians">
        Waldensian
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/La_nobla_leyczon" title="La nobla leyczon">
         La nobla leyczon
        </a>
       </i>
       (dated 1100),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        la
        <a href="/wiki/Can%C3%A7%C3%B3_de_Santa_Fe" title="Cançó de Santa Fe">
         Cançó de Santa Fe
        </a>
       </i>
       (ca 1054–1076), the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Romance_of_Flamenca" title="Romance of Flamenca">
         Romance of Flamenca
        </a>
       </i>
       (13th century), the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Song_of_the_Albigensian_Crusade" title="Song of the Albigensian Crusade">
         Song of the Albigensian Crusade
        </a>
       </i>
       (1213–1219?),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Daurel_e_Bet%C3%B3" title="Daurel e Betó">
         Daurel e Betó
        </a>
       </i>
       (12th or 13th century),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Las,_qu%27i_non_sun_sparvir,_astur" title="Las, qu'i non sun sparvir, astur">
         Las, qu'i non sun sparvir, astur
        </a>
       </i>
       (11th century) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tomida_femina" title="Tomida femina">
         Tomida femina
        </a>
       </i>
       (9th or 10th century).
      </p>
      <p>
       Occitan was the vehicle for the influential
       <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">
        poetry
       </a>
       of the medieval
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troubadours" title="Troubadours">
        troubadours
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        trovadores
       </i>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Trobairitz" title="Trobairitz">
        trobairises
       </a>
       : At that time, the language was understood and celebrated throughout most of educated Europe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was the maternal language of the English queen
       <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitaine" title="Eleanor of Aquitaine">
        Eleanor of Aquitaine
       </a>
       and kings
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">
        Richard I of England
       </a>
       (who wrote troubadour poetry) and
       <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">
        John, King of England
       </a>
       . With the gradual imposition of French royal power over its territory, Occitan declined in status from the 14th century on. By the
       <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Villers-Cotter%C3%AAts" title="Ordinance of Villers-Cotterêts">
        Ordinance of Villers-Cotterêts
       </a>
       (1539) it was decreed that the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langue d'oïl">
         langue d'oïl
        </a>
       </i>
       (French) should be used for all French administration. Occitan's greatest decline was during the
       <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
        French Revolution
       </a>
       , during which diversity of language was considered a threat.
      </p>
      <p>
       The literary renaissance of the late 19th century (which included a Nobel Prize for Frédéric Mistral) was attenuated by
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       , when Occitan speakers spent extended periods of time alongside French-speaking comrades.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">
        Origins
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origins">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linguistic_map_Southwestern_Europe.gif">
         <img alt="Linguistic map Southwestern Europe.gif" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="600" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Linguistic_map_Southwestern_Europe.gif/300px-Linguistic_map_Southwestern_Europe.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Linguistic_map_Southwestern_Europe.gif/450px-Linguistic_map_Southwestern_Europe.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/Linguistic_map_Southwestern_Europe.gif 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linguistic_map_Southwestern_Europe.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Because the geographical territory in which Occitan is spoken is surrounded by regions in which other
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       are used, external influences could have influenced its origin and development. Many factors favoured its development as a language of its own.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Mountains and seas: The range of Occitan is bounded naturally by the
        <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">
         Mediterranean Sea
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">
         Atlantic Ocean
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Massif_Central" title="Massif Central">
         Massif Central
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">
         Pyrenees
        </a>
        , and the
        <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">
         Alps
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Buffer zones:
        <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">
         Arid
        </a>
        land,
        <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">
         marshes
        </a>
        , and areas otherwise impractical for farming and resistant of colonization provide further separation (territory between
        <a href="/wiki/Loire" title="Loire">
         Loire
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Garonne" title="Garonne">
         Garonne
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">
         Aragon
        </a>
        desert plateau).
       </li>
       <li>
        Constant populations: Some Occitan-speaking peoples are descended from people living in the region since prehistory
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBec1963_27-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBec1963-27">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Little
        <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">
         Celtic
        </a>
        influence
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBec1963_27-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBec1963-27">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Ancient and long-term Roman influence:
        <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">
         Julius Caesar
        </a>
        once said that the people of
        <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">
         Aquitaine
        </a>
        could teach the
        <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
         Romans
        </a>
        themselves to speak Latin more correctly. According to Müller, "France's linguistic separation began with Roman influence"
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBec196320-21_28-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBec196320-21-28">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        A separate
        <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">
         lexicon
        </a>
        : Although Occitan is midway between the
        <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Romance_languages" title="Gallo-Romance languages">
         Gallo-Romance
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Romance_languages" title="Iberian Romance languages">
         Iberian Romance languages
        </a>
        , it has "around 550 words inherited from Latin that do not exist in the
        <a href="/wiki/Langues_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langues d'oïl">
         langues d'oïl
        </a>
        or in
        <a href="/wiki/Franco-Proven%C3%A7al_language" title="Franco-Provençal language">
         Franco-Provençal
        </a>
        "
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBec196320-21_28-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBec196320-21-28">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Little germanization: "The
        <a href="/wiki/Frankish_language" title="Frankish language">
         Frankish
        </a>
        lexicon and its phonetic influence often end above the oc/oïl line"
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBec196320–21_29-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBec196320–21-29">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Occitan_in_the_Iberian_Peninsula">
        Occitan in the Iberian Peninsula
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Occitan in the Iberian Peninsula">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Catalan in Spain's northern and central Mediterranean coastal regions and the
       <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">
        Balearic Islands
       </a>
       is closely related to Occitan, sharing many linguistic features and a common origin (see
       <a href="/wiki/Occitano-Romance_languages" title="Occitano-Romance languages">
        Occitano-Romance languages
       </a>
       ). The language was one of the first to gain prestige as a medium for literature among Romance languages in the Middle Ages. Indeed, in the 12th and 13th centuries, Catalan troubadours such as Guerau de Cabrera, Guilhem de Bergadan, Guilhem de Cabestany, Huguet de Mataplana, Raimon Vidal de Besalú, Cerverí de Girona, Formit de Perpinhan, and Jofre de Foixà wrote in Occitan.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the end of the 11th century, the
       <i>
        Franks
       </i>
       , as they were called at the time, started to penetrate the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
        Iberian Peninsula
       </a>
       through the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Way_of_St._James" title="Way of St. James">
        Ways of St. James
       </a>
       via
       <a href="/wiki/Somport" title="Somport">
        Somport
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Roncesvalles" title="Roncesvalles">
        Roncesvalles
       </a>
       , settling on various spots of the Kingdoms of
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">
        Navarre
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">
        Aragon
       </a>
       enticed by the privileges granted them by the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Navarrese_monarchs" title="List of Navarrese monarchs">
        Navarrese kings
       </a>
       . They established themselves in ethnic
       <a href="/wiki/Borough" title="Borough">
        boroughs
       </a>
       where Occitan was used for everyday life, e.g. Pamplona,
       <a href="/wiki/Sang%C3%BCesa" title="Sangüesa">
        Sangüesa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Estella-Lizarra" title="Estella-Lizarra">
        Estella-Lizarra
       </a>
       , etc.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The language in turn became the status language chosen by the Navarrese kings, nobility and upper classes for official and trade purposes in the period stretching from the early 13th century to late 14th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These boroughs in Navarre may have been close-knit communities with little mingling, in a context where the natural milieu was predominantly
       <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">
        Basque
       </a>
       -speaking. The variant chosen for written administrative records was a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">
         koiné
        </a>
       </i>
       based on the Languedocien dialect from Toulouse with fairly archaic linguistic features.
      </p>
      <p>
       Evidence of a written account in Occitan from Pamplona revolving around the burning of borough San Nicolas from 1258 survives today, while the
       <i>
        History of the War of Navarre
       </i>
       by Guilhem Anelier (1276) albeit written in Pamplona shows a linguistic variant from
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Toulouse#13th_to_14th_century" title="History of Toulouse">
        Toulouse
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Things turned out slightly otherwise in Aragon, where the sociolinguistic situation was different, with a clearer Basque-Romance bilingual situation (cf. Basques from the Val d'Aran cited c. 1000), but a receding Basque language (Basque banned in the marketplace of Huesca, 1349).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the language was chosen as a medium of prestige in records and official statements along with Latin in the early 13th century, Occitan faced competition from the rising local Romance vernacular, the
       <a href="/wiki/Navarro-Aragonese" title="Navarro-Aragonese">
        Navarro-Aragonese
       </a>
       , both orally and in writing, especially after Aragon's territorial conquests south to
       <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">
        Zaragoza
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Huesca" title="Huesca">
        Huesca
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tudela,_Navarre" title="Tudela, Navarre">
        Tudela
       </a>
       between 1118 and 1134. It resulted that a second Occitan immigration of this period was assimilated by the similar Navarro-Aragonese language, which at the same time was fostered and chosen by the kings of
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">
        Aragon
       </a>
       . The language fell into decay in the 14th century across the whole southern Pyrenean area and became largely absorbed into Navarro-Aragonese first and
       <a href="/wiki/Castilian_Spanish" title="Castilian Spanish">
        Castilian
       </a>
       later in the 15th century, after their exclusive boroughs broke up (1423,
       <a href="/wiki/Pamplona#Three_boroughs_and_one_city" title="Pamplona">
        Pamplona
       </a>
       's boroughs unified).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Gascon-speaking communities were called in for trading purposes by Navarrese kings in the early 12th century to the coastal fringe extending from
       <a href="/wiki/San_Sebasti%C3%A1n" title="San Sebastián">
        San Sebastian
       </a>
       to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bidasoa_River" title="Bidasoa River">
        Bidasoa River
       </a>
       , where they settled down. The language variant used was different from the ones used in Navarre, i.e. a
       <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9arnese_dialect" title="Béarnese dialect">
        Béarnese dialect
       </a>
       of Gascon,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dialnet.unirioja.es_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dialnet.unirioja.es-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with Gascon being in use far longer than in Navarre and Aragon until the 19th century, thanks mainly to the close ties held by Donostia and
       <a href="/wiki/Pasaia" title="Pasaia">
        Pasaia
       </a>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Bayonne" title="Bayonne">
        Bayonne
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">
        Geographic distribution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Usage_in_France">
        Usage in France
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Usage in France">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           does not
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
            cite
           </a>
           any
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            sources
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Occitan_language&amp;action=edit">

            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">

           </a>

          </span>

           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
